This is not metaphorical. In the Vedic framework, chanting the beeja mantra of the Sun one hundred and eight times on a Sunday morning is a relational act — an address to a living intelligence, not a psychological exercise.

Each of the twenty-seven Nakshatras possesses a specific *Shakti* — a power. Ashwini carries the power to quickly heal. Bharani carries the power of transformation through death and rebirth. Krittika carries the power to burn and purify. Each Shakti has a "basis above," a "basis below," and a "result" forming a triadic structure that maps onto tantric cosmology.

· · ·  
The most explicit bridge between astrology and tantra runs through the Dashamahavidya — the Ten Great Wisdom Goddesses, central to Shakta Tantra. The Todala Tantra assigns each goddess to a specific Graha: Kali governs Saturn, Tara governs Jupiter, Tripura Sundari governs Mercury, Bhuvaneshvari governs Moon, Chinnamasta governs Rahu, Dhumavati governs Ketu, Bagalamukhi governs Mars, Matangi governs Sun, Kamala governs Venus. Worship of the corresponding Mahavidya is prescribed as a tantric remedial measure for planetary afflictions — qualitatively different from standard Vedic remedies because it involves full tantric sadhana.

The concept of Kala — both "time" and "death" — may be the deepest bridge. Mahakala, Great Time, is a form of Shiva representing eternity beyond cyclic time. Mahakali is his Shakti counterpart. Manifest time — *kala* — is what Jyotish maps. Unmanifest time — Mahakala — is what tantric realization aims to penetrate. The astrologer reads the clock. The tantric practitioner asks who is doing the reading.

· · ·  
Christopher Wallis's non-dual Shaiva Tantra offers a framing that no other platform attempts. In the Pratyabhijña tradition, all phenomena — including planetary influences — are expressions of *Cit-Shakti*, consciousness-power. The planets are not external forces controlling us but aspects of awareness to recognize within ourselves. Practice becomes *pratyabhijñā* — recognition — rather than propitiation.

This framing resolves a tension that plagues most digital astrology: the fine line between insight and superstition. By grounding Jyotish in non-dual philosophy — where "the planets in the sky" and "the planets in consciousness" are recognized as non-different — a platform can offer genuine depth without the ethical problems of fortune-telling. The grammars become contemplative mirrors rather than predictive instruments.

And this is not mysticism requiring special belief. It is a phenomenological claim about the structure of experience — compatible with Bohm's implicate order, with Merleau-Ponty's embodied perception, with Thomas Csordas's "somatic modes of attention." The claim is simple: when you attend to Saturn — in your chart, in the sky, in your experience of limitation and discipline and time — something responds. Not because Saturn is a god beaming influence at you. But because consciousness, attending to a quality of itself, recognizes what was always already there.

Grammars are not dead symbol systems. They are invitations to enter into living relationship with a responsive cosmos. Whether you frame that response as divine agency, as synchronicity, as the projective capacity of the human psyche, or as the implicate order unfolding into the explicate — the phenomenological experience is the same. You cast. Something is cast back. You attend. Something attends to you.

The grammar is the space where the meeting happens.

Chapter Eight

## The Commons

Marsha Linehan developed the biosocial hypothesis — and the dialectical framework that grew from it — not from ideology but because it was effective, fit the data, and was compassionate. She had tried everything with patients who were suicidal, who had been failed by every existing treatment. Cognitive behavioral therapy alone wasn't enough. Psychodynamic therapy alone wasn't enough. She arrived at dialectics — holding opposites in creative tension, accepting reality while simultaneously working to change it — because the people in front of her needed it. Three criteria guided her: Does it work? Does the evidence support it? Does it reduce suffering?

I want to apply those three criteria to a different problem. AI has arrived. Social structures are fraying. People are drowning in digital consumption but starving for authentic creation and connection. We need to build culture faster — not the culture of content and consumption, but the culture of meaning-making, of contemplative practice, of people creating together rather than consuming alone.

What organizational form is effective, fits the evidence, and is compassionate?

· · ·  
*Effective.*

The evidence that open, community-maintained infrastructure outperforms closed alternatives at scale is now overwhelming. Linux runs virtually every server on the internet, every Android phone, one hundred percent of the world's top five hundred supercomputers. Android powers roughly 3.9 billion active devices. Wikipedia is the world's encyclopedia. Ghost — the publishing platform founded by John O'Nolan as a nonprofit under MIT license — generates nearly nine million dollars annually with no investors, no owners, zero percent transaction fees, and all revenue reinvested. Supabase, built entirely from open-source components, reached a five billion dollar valuation in five years. The pattern repeats: when knowledge is shared, it multiplies.

The key structural insight, documented by researchers analyzing forty-four open-source developer tools, is that business model choice predicts commercial outcomes more reliably than community adoption metrics. Projects controlling service-layer infrastructure achieve valuations orders of magnitude higher than community-funded alternatives with comparable community engagement. Control of distribution and operational infrastructure matters more than control of code.

This is the open-core model: open-source the foundation, sustain the platform through hosted services. Ghost does this. Supabase does this. The foundation is free and modifiable. The service layer generates revenue. The mission is protected by the legal structure — a nonprofit foundation, in Ghost's case, that legally prevents being sold, pivoted, or pressured by shareholders.

· · ·  
*Fits the evidence.*

In 2008, the anthropologist Christopher Kelty published *Two Bits: The Cultural Significance of Free Software* — itself under a Creative Commons license, embodying the principles it described. Kelty's central theoretical contribution was the concept of the *recursive public*:

*A recursive public is a public that is vitally concerned with the material and practical maintenance and modification of the technical, legal, practical, and conceptual means of its own existence as a public; it is a collective independent of other forms of constituted power and is capable of speaking to existing forms of power through the production of actually existing alternatives.*  
What distinguishes a recursive public from an interest group, a corporation, a church, or a movement is its focus on the radical modifiability of its own terms of existence. The community does not just use the medium to communicate. It creates and maintains that medium as a form of political action.

Kelty identified five interlocking practices that constitute free software as a recursive public: sharing source code, conceiving open systems, writing copyright licenses, coordinating collaboration, and proselytizing. Each practice has its own history, its own tensions, its own experiments. Together they form what Kelty calls "a system of thresholds, not of classification" — an experimental system whose infrastructure is the Internet and whose hypotheses concern the reorientation of knowledge and power.

Kelty's concept was developed to describe software. But he anticipated its extension. In Part III of *Two Bits*, he examined Creative Commons and the Connexions open textbook project as "modulations" of free software — instances where the five practices were adopted and transformed for new domains. He asked: do these modulations maintain the orientation of a recursive public? Do they extend the commitment to availability and modifiability into education, scholarship, cultural production?

I am asking the same question for contemplative practice.

· · ·  
The five components, modulated:

*Sharing grammars* modulates sharing source code. The grammars — tarot decks, I Ching hexagrams, Nakshatras, Dhammapada verses, children's stories, wellness exercises — are the "source code" of Jongu. They are created by people, not algorithms. They are shareable, forkable, modifiable. Anyone can create a grammar. Anyone can contribute to an existing one. The platform makes every grammar publicly and freely available.

*Conceiving openness in sacred systems* modulates conceiving open systems. This is the hardest modulation. "Open" means something different when the content is a Yoruba divination system transmitted through initiated lineages than when it is a software protocol. The CARE Principles for Indigenous Data Governance — Collective Benefit, Authority to Control, Responsibility, Ethics — provide the ethical framework. Some grammars are structurally open (tarot, I Ching) and belong in the commons. Others are initiated, oral, and lineage-dependent (Ifá) and should be represented on the platform only with the explicit involvement and authority of tradition-holders. The principle is: gateway building, not gatekeeping — but the gateway must be built with the community's consent.

*Writing the license* modulates writing copyright licenses. Creative Commons Attribution-ShareAlike 4.0 International. The SA — ShareAlike — is the contemplative equivalent of copyleft. Anyone who builds on the grammars must share their derivatives under the same terms. The freedom propagates. The code for the platform itself is open-source. The content created by the community is commons. What is proprietary is nothing.

*Coordinating co-creation* modulates coordinating collaboration. The Discord server. The blog. The community voting on features, contributing tools, creating channels. The Jongo dance as metaphor — participants taking turns leading and supporting, individual expression within a collective rhythm. The design principle from the structured disagreement research: not resolving conflicts but building the capacity to hold them. Polis's insight that removing the reply button and surfacing consensus transforms discourse from combat to inquiry.

*The movement* modulates proselytizing. This book. The blog posts. The landing page. The argument — not for a specific metaphysics, but for a structural claim: humans need non-dogmatic, non-revealed symbolic systems to make meaning, and these systems should be commons, not commodities. The argument is made not through rhetoric but through the production of actually existing alternatives. The platform exists. The grammars are available. The community is forming. The proof is in the practice.

· · ·  
*Compassionate.*

The alternative is the attention economy. Co-Star sending push notifications designed to generate engagement anxiety. Headspace charging seventy dollars a year for access to meditation — a practice that has been freely transmitted for twenty-five hundred years. Labyrinthos gamifying tarot into streaks and achievement badges. The three-hundred-and-seventy-six-billion-dollar spiritual services industry extracting value from people's desire for meaning.

A commons is compassionate because it makes meaning-making infrastructure available to everyone — not as a product to consume but as a practice to participate in. A ten-dollar tool feels very different to someone in Brazil than to someone in Silicon Valley. When access isn't tied to purchasing power, people can focus on genuine contribution rather than financial transactions.

Jongu's architecture already embodies this. No ads. No algorithm. No notifications. No tracking. Privacy by design — you can use every tool without creating an account. Optional AI — assistance is always opt-in, never required. Donation-sustained access — one hundred percent free, sustained by voluntary contribution. Everything exportable, everything deletable, everything yours.

The platform does not monetize attention. It does not gamify practice. It does not claim authority over your experience. It holds your question without answering it.

· · ·  
Kelty quotes Creative Commons with the phrase "If we succeed, we will disappear." The idea is that if the licenses work — if they become the default infrastructure through which culture is shared — then the organization that created them becomes invisible. The licenses are just there, like plumbing.

The same applies to Jongu. The goal is not to build a company. The goal is not even to build a platform. The goal is to build an infrastructure that becomes so fundamental it becomes invisible — the way Wikipedia became invisible, the way Linux became invisible, the way the protocols that run the Internet became invisible. Not noticed, not branded, not owned. Just there. Available. Modifiable. Maintained by the community that uses it.

Kelty called this "adaptability over planning." The commitment to remain modifiable — to build in the capacity to be changed by the community — rather than to plan a fixed product and defend it. This is the opposite of how platforms are normally built. Normally you plan the product, build it in secret, launch it, defend it, scale it, sell it. The recursive public does something different. It builds the infrastructure in public. It invites modification. It treats every version as a draft. It privileges the community's capacity to adapt over the founder's capacity to plan.

This is also, I realize, what the grammars themselves do. The I Ching has been continuously modified for three thousand years — from oracle bones to the Ten Wings to Wang Bi's commentary to Zhu Xi's reconstruction to the Wilhelm-Baynes translation to whatever version you cast this morning. Tarot has been continuously modified since 1440 — from Visconti-Sforza to Court de Gébelin to the Golden Dawn to Pamela Colman Smith to Rachel Pollack to Cristy C. Road to whoever designs the next deck tomorrow. Ifá has been continuously modified through every Babalawo who commits a new verse to memory and every diaspora community that adapts the practice to new circumstances.

The grammars are recursive publics. They always have been. They are communities of practitioners who build, maintain, and modify the very symbolic infrastructure through which they make meaning. What changes now is the speed and scale at which this can happen. AI makes it possible to create a new grammar — a new tarot deck, a new set of contemplative prompts, a new children's story — in hours rather than years. The platform makes it possible to share that grammar with everyone on earth, instantly, for free.

The hypothesis is that this acceleration is a gift, not a threat — but only if the infrastructure is held as commons. If the infrastructure is held privately, the acceleration serves the attention economy. If it is held as commons, the acceleration serves the community.

· · ·  
This is an experiment. It might work. It might not. But the hypothesis is clear, the evidence is substantial, and the need is urgent.

Grammars have survived millennia through compactness, openness, and the irreducible participation of practitioners. AI is here, and we need to build culture faster. The recursive public is the form that fits. The commons is the license that protects. The grammars are the source code.

Everything else is practice.

This work is licensed under Creative Commons Attribution-ShareAlike 4.0 International (CC BY-SA 4.0).  
You are free to share and adapt this material for any purpose, including commercially,  
under the terms that you give appropriate credit and distribute your contributions  
under the same license.

*How this book was made*

This book was drafted in conversation with Claude, an AI made by Anthropic.  
The research, the platform, and the argument belong to playfulProcess.  
The prose was assembled in a single sitting from years of prior work.  
Neither party claims authorship. Both contributed labor.

AI requires enormous energy to run. This book is released as creative commons  
because there are externalities in every conversation with an AI,  
and the only honest response is to give the output back.  
If the energy was spent, let the knowledge be shared.

The book that describes the recursive public should itself be recursively public.

*jongu.org  ·  recursive.eco  ·  playfulprocess.com*

# Research 1

# **Research 1: Fred Rogers as Media Philosopher**

**Serves:** Chapters 2 and 8 of *Grammars of the Living World* **Compiled:** March 2026 **License:** CC BY-SA 4.0 (consistent with the book's own license)

---

## **1\. Rogers's Theology and How It Shaped His Media Philosophy**

Fred McFeely Rogers (1928–2003) was ordained a Presbyterian minister in 1963 by the Pittsburgh Presbytery at Third Presbyterian Church — with the highly unusual charge of conducting his ministry through mass media directed at children and families. This was not metaphorical. Rogers understood his television work as literal ministry. He walked into the WQED studio each day saying, "Dear God, let some word that is heard be Yours."

### **The Seminary Path**

Rogers enrolled at Pittsburgh Theological Seminary in 1954, attending classes on his lunch breaks while working at WQED. He graduated with a Bachelor of Divinity in 1962\. His theological formation was Presbyterian — rooted in the Reformed tradition — but his personal reading and practice were far more ecumenical. Maxwell King's biography *The Good Neighbor* (2018) documents that Rogers was "a student of Catholic mysticism, Buddhism, Judaism, and other faiths." Multiple observers noted "an almost Zen-like quality in the pace of his work and his life." The books found on his bedside table at his death ranged across religious and philosophical traditions.

This ecumenical formation matters for the book's argument. Rogers did not approach children from a sectarian framework. He approached them from something closer to what Tillich would call "ultimate concern" — the conviction that every child possesses inherent worth and dignity, and that the task of the adult is to communicate that conviction through every available medium. His theology was incarnational: the love of God made present not through doctrine but through presence, attention, and the willingness to take children's inner lives seriously.

### **The Charge as Ministry**

The Fred Rogers Institute states it plainly: "Fred Rogers had a profoundly unique commission as an ordained minister: to serve children and families through public media." Rogers himself said: "When I was ordained, it was for a special ministry, that of serving children and families through television. I consider that what I do through Mister Rogers' Neighborhood is my ministry. A ministry doesn't have to be only through a church, or even through an ordination."

**Key insight for the book:** Rogers never pastored a brick-and-mortar church. His parish was the television screen. This is directly analogous to what Jongu/Recursive.eco attempts — using a digital medium as the infrastructure for contemplative practice. Rogers proved that a technological medium could serve the inner life rather than exploit it. The question the book asks is whether a platform can do the same for grammars.

---

## **2\. Specific Design Decisions for *Mister Rogers' Neighborhood***

### **Pace as Philosophy**

The most distinctive design feature of *Mister Rogers' Neighborhood* was its deliberately slow pace. In a study by McCollum & Bryant (2003) that coded 85 popular children's shows for pacing — defined as frequency of scene changes, camera cuts, auditory changes, talking, music, and motion — *Mighty Morphin' Power Rangers* scored 41.90 (among the fastest) and *Mister Rogers' Neighborhood* scored 14.95 (the slowest of all shows measured).

This was not accidental. As King writes, "Rogers required a slower pace and smooth delivery to show children that their pace was the right pace to learn." The deliberate slowness communicated a philosophical claim: that the child's tempo of engagement is valuable, that rushing is a form of disrespect, and that real understanding requires time.

### **Direct Address and the "Television Neighbor"**

Rogers looked directly into the camera and spoke to the child as though they were in the same room. This was revolutionary in children's television and drew from his theological conviction about presence. The technique created what media scholars call a "parasocial relationship" — but Rogers understood it as something more fundamental: the capacity of a medium to communicate genuine care across distance.

### **Real-Time Activities**

Rogers performed activities in real time — changing his shoes, feeding his fish, visiting a factory. He did not use jump cuts or time compression. This served multiple developmental purposes: it modeled patience, demonstrated that processes have beginnings and endings, and allowed children to process what they were seeing at their own cognitive pace.

### **The Distinction Between Reality and Fantasy**

Margaret McFarland urged Rogers to appear on camera himself, telling him: "Fred, the children need to see you. They need you to help them distinguish between reality and fantasy." The show's structure — the transition from the "real" neighborhood to the Neighborhood of Make-Believe via Trolley — was a deliberate architectural device for this purpose. Rogers always made the boundary explicit. This is a design principle directly relevant to AI-age platforms: the ethical obligation to make clear what is real and what is constructed.

### **Inclusion of Mistakes**

Rogers chose to include mistakes ("follies") in his productions rather than editing them out. This communicated that perfection is not required for learning or for being valued — a direct expression of his theological conviction about unconditional acceptance.

### **Reading on Camera**

When anyone read aloud on the show, the camera showed the words and tracked from left to right to mimic the path of the eyes in reading. As a former producer noted: "All those little tiny details were really important to Fred." Every production choice served the child's developmental needs, not the adult's aesthetic preferences.

---

## **3\. The 1969 Senate Testimony**

On May 1, 1969, Rogers testified before the U.S. Senate Commerce Subcommittee on Communications, chaired by Senator John Pastore of Rhode Island. President Nixon's administration had proposed cutting federal funding for public broadcasting from $20 million to $10 million amid Vietnam War costs.

Pastore was known as gruff and impatient. He had never heard of Rogers or his show. In approximately six minutes of testimony, Rogers:

* Explained that $6,000 (his per-show budget) pays for less than two minutes of cartoons — "two minutes of animated, what I sometimes say, bombardment"  
* Described the show's emphasis on helping children manage feelings, particularly anger  
* Recited the lyrics to "What Do You Do with the Mad That You Feel?" — a song teaching children that having angry feelings is normal, and what matters is what you do with them

Pastore's response is now legendary: "I'm supposed to be a pretty tough guy, and this is the first time I've had goosebumps for the last two days. I think it's wonderful. I think it's wonderful. Looks like you just earned the $20 million."

Congressional funding for PBS subsequently increased from $9 million to $22 million. In 1970, Nixon appointed Rogers as chair of the White House Conference on Children and Youth. King's biography describes the testimony as "considered one of the most powerful pieces of testimony ever offered before Congress."

**Key insight for the book:** Rogers did not lobby for funding by citing audience numbers or production values. He described a philosophy of care and demonstrated it through his own presence. The medium *was* the message. The testimony has resurfaced in 2012, 2017, and 2025 whenever PBS funding faces political threat — suggesting that Rogers's argument about the irreducibility of care in public media remains as urgent as ever.

---

## **4\. The Margaret McFarland Collaboration**

### **Who She Was**

Margaret Beall McFarland (1905–1988) was a developmental psychologist at the University of Pittsburgh School of Medicine and co-founder of the Arsenal Family and Children's Center — alongside Benjamin Spock and Erik Erikson. Erikson said of her: "Margaret McFarland knew more than anyone in this world about families with young children."

McFarland received her PhD from Columbia University, taught in Melbourne, and returned to Pittsburgh where she became an associate professor in the Department of Psychiatry. She wrote almost nothing — her dissertation and a single journal article on "development of motherliness." Her influence was transmitted entirely through relationships, particularly her 30-year collaboration with Rogers.

### **The Nature of Their Collaboration**

Rogers and McFarland met weekly — sometimes daily — from the late 1950s until her death in 1988\. She reviewed every script. She helped him understand the developmental dimensions of each topic he wanted to address. Rogers took extensive handwritten notes and recorded their conversations on audiocassettes — recordings that survive in the Fred Rogers Archive at Saint Vincent College.

**The fire story is essential for the book.** Rogers wanted to do a program about fire. McFarland "helped me to realize that it was essential to deal with control of fluids before even introducing anything about fire. I learned, for instance, that most children's dreams about fire center around their control of their own body fluids\!" This is the kind of developmental depth that no algorithm can replicate — and the kind of care that Jongu's architecture must find a way to honor.

### **Her Teaching Philosophy**

McFarland believed that children "caught" attitudes from teachers rather than being taught them directly. Once, when a sculptor from Carnegie Mellon visited the Arsenal Center, she told him: "I don't want you to teach sculpting. All I want you to do is to love clay in front of the children." The children caught his enthusiasm.

She also originated the phrase that Rogers made famous: "Anything human is mentionable, and anything mentionable is manageable." This became the philosophical spine of the Neighborhood.

**The "never to Fred; always with Fred" line** (from King's biography, quoting McFarland's conversational style) captures something essential about how she modeled relational presence. She did not lecture Rogers. She thought alongside him. This is the quality that distinguishes contemplative dialogue from instruction — and the quality that Jongu's "grammars" attempt to hold space for.

---

## **5\. Christakis's Research: Content, Pace, and Attention**

### **The Core Finding**

Dr. Dimitri Christakis (Seattle Children's Research Institute, editor-in-chief of *JAMA Pediatrics*) and his colleagues have produced the most consequential body of research on television's effects on child development. Their landmark 2004 study found that early television exposure was associated with attentional problems at age 7\.

Critically, a 2007 follow-up by Zimmerman and Christakis found that this association was specific to non-educational viewing and to viewing before age 3\. Educational programming (including Rogers-style content) showed no adverse cognitive outcomes. The distinction is between *what* children watch, not merely *how much*.

### **The Mouse Model**

Christakis partnered with neuroscientist Nino Ramirez to test the pacing hypothesis experimentally. Mice exposed to 6 hours daily of fast-paced audiovisual stimulation (rapid scene changes, intense color and audio shifts) for 42 days during a sensitive developmental period subsequently performed worse on behavioral and cognitive tests, were hyperactive, and failed to show species-typical caution — compared to control mice. The rapid changes in visual and auditory stimuli during a period of rapid synaptic growth and pruning were hypothesized to explain these effects.

### **The Content-Pace Interaction**

The research landscape shows a crucial nuance: it is not pace alone but the interaction of pace, content, and realism that matters. Lillard and Peterson (2011) found that fast-paced fantastical programming impaired executive function in 4-year-olds. A later study (Lillard et al., 2015\) suggested the fantastical content mattered more than pace per se. Kostyrka-Allchorne et al. (2019) found that realism may buffer against the negative effects of rapid pace.

What emerges is a picture where Rogers-style content — slow-paced, realistic, directly addressed to the child, grounded in real activities and genuine emotions — represents the opposite pole from the content associated with attentional harm.

### **Rogers Knew This Intuitively**

Christakis has noted in interviews that Rogers understood principles his research would later confirm. Rogers's shows asked questions of children and paused for responses — creating a sense of contingent interaction that predates touchscreen interactivity by decades. As Christakis observed: "Rogers knew that... asking questions would make the children attend to the screen and the children would answer the question and then invariably the answer was, 'That's right\!'" This contingent responsiveness — the child feels heard — is what makes Rogers-style programming developmentally beneficial and what makes engagement-optimized apps developmentally harmful.

---

## **6\. The Daniel Tiger Research**

### **The Study Design**

*Daniel Tiger's Neighborhood* (PBS Kids, from the Fred Rogers Company) is the direct descendant of *Mister Rogers' Neighborhood*, targeting the same 2–5 age group with a social-emotional development curriculum. Researchers at Texas Tech University (Rasmussen et al., 2016\) enrolled 127 preschoolers and their parents in a controlled study:

* **Group 1:** Watched *Daniel Tiger* with parents; parents actively discussed the show  
* **Group 2:** Watched the show with parents; no discussion  
* **Group 3:** Watched the show alone; no discussion  
* **Control:** Watched nature documentary episodes

After two weeks of daily 30-minute viewing, children were tested on empathy, emotion recognition, and self-efficacy (confidence in social situations).

### **The Results**

Children who watched *Daniel Tiger's Neighborhood* exhibited higher levels of empathy, were better at recognizing emotions, and were more confident in social situations than the control group. **But the empathy scores were only significant in the group where parents participated in active mediation** — watching together and discussing the content.

A subsequent study (published in *Media Psychology*, 2018\) found that children who played with the *Daniel Tiger* app used the emotion regulation strategies taught by the show more frequently one month later. Preschoolers (3- and 4-year-olds) showed higher levels of emotion knowledge after app use. Watching the TV episodes at home also increased parents' own provision of active mediation — suggesting the show teaches parents, not just children.

### **The Critical Takeaway**

**The parental presence finding is the most important result for the book.** It confirms what Rogers and McFarland understood intuitively: media cannot substitute for relationship. The medium serves the child only when it is embedded in a relational context — a parent who watches, discusses, reinforces. This directly supports Jongu's design philosophy of co-viewing, co-creation, and shared practice rather than passive consumption. The grammar is the space where the meeting happens — but only if someone else is in the room.

As Rasmussen said: "It's not enough to just plop your kid in front of the TV and hope they're going to develop these social and emotional skills. There has to be a certain level of involvement in kids' TV viewing experiences."

---

## **7\. Rogers's Third Way: Neither Technophobia nor Techno-Utopianism**

Rogers's approach to television represents a position that the book's Chapter 7 ("The Trap of Solving") needs as its central case study. He was neither anti-television nor a naïve believer in technology's power to save. He saw what television was doing to children in the 1960s — "pies in faces and slapstick" — and was "appalled." But his response was not to reject the medium. It was to demonstrate what the medium looked like when it served the child.

This is the exact logic that Recursive.eco applies to digital platforms:

* **The attention economy is real.** Co-Star sending push notifications. Headspace charging $70/year for meditation. Labyrinthos gamifying tarot into streaks.  
* **The response is not to reject technology.** It is to build technology that serves contemplative practice rather than exploiting it.  
* **The proof is in the practice.** Rogers did not argue for better television. He made better television. The platform should not argue for better digital contemplation. It should provide it.

Rogers's own use of the word "modulations" is uncanny in the context of Kelty's theoretical framework. In the opening scene of the documentary *Won't You Be My Neighbor?*, a young Rogers sits at a piano and explains: "It seems to me that there are different themes in life. And one of my main jobs, it seems to me, is to help, through the mass media for children, to help children through some of the difficult modulations of life. Because it's easy for instance to go from C to F. But there are some modulations that aren't so easy. For instance, to go from F to F\#, you've got to weave through all sorts of things. And it seems to me if you've got somebody to help you, as you weave…"

**Rogers was describing modulation in the musical and developmental sense — but the word resonates with Kelty's use of "modulation" to describe what happens when the practices of free software are extended into new domains.** Jongu/Recursive.eco is a modulation of both: it takes Rogers's modulation of television into care and Kelty's modulation of software into commons and asks what happens when both are applied to contemplative practice.

---

## **8\. Hedda Sharapan and the Production Philosophy**

Hedda Sharapan was Rogers's longtime associate at Family Communications, Inc. (now Fred Rogers Productions), working with him for over 30 years. She has served as the primary keeper of his production philosophy in the years since his death, articulating the principles that guided every creative decision on the show.

The production philosophy can be summarized in several principles that map directly onto Jongu's design:

1. **Respect the child's pace.** Never rush. Never condescend. Never assume the child cannot handle real feelings.  
2. **Make the invisible visible.** Show processes. Show how things are made. Show what happens inside people when they feel things.  
3. **Hold questions without answering them.** Rogers asked children what they thought. He paused. He let silence do its work.  
4. **Ground every creative choice in developmental science.** The McFarland collaboration was not decoration. It was the method.  
5. **The medium serves the child, not the other way around.** No ratings chasing. No engagement optimization. No dark patterns.

---

## **9\. Connections to the Book's Architecture**

### **For Chapter 2 (The Crisis of Children's Media)**

Rogers serves as the positive pole in a chapter that documents the crisis. If the attention economy represents what happens when media exploits children's developmental vulnerabilities, Rogers represents what happens when media honors them. The Christakis research provides the empirical bridge: fast-paced entertainment content harms developing attention systems; slow-paced, realistic, emotionally grounded content does not — and may actively help, especially when accompanied by parental engagement.

### **For Chapter 8 (The Commons / Grammars of the Living World)**

Rogers is the emotional and structural model for Jongu's design philosophy. The book's closing chapter should open with the Kelty concept of the recursive public, but it should *feel* like Rogers — warm, serious, respectful of the reader's intelligence, unwilling to simplify. The closing line from Kelty — "If we succeed, we will disappear" — maps onto Rogers's invisibility. He did not build a brand. He built a presence. The goal of the platform is the same: infrastructure so fundamental it becomes invisible.

### **The "Modulations" Connection**

Rogers using the word "modulations" to describe his work with children, and Kelty using the same word to describe how free software practices extend into new domains, is a remarkable convergence. The book should let this resonate without forcing it. Rogers was modulating television. Kelty was modulating software. Jongu modulates both into contemplative practice. The grammars are the source code. The commons is the license. The community is the public. Everything else is practice.

---

## **Key Sources for Further Reading**

* **Maxwell King**, *The Good Neighbor: The Life and Work of Fred Rogers* (Abrams, 2018\) — the definitive biography  
* **Margaret McFarland** — Pitt Med feature "When Fred Met Margaret"; Wikipedia "Overlooked" profile in NYT; Mister Rogers' Neighborhood official site  
* **Christakis et al.** (2004), "Early Television Exposure and Subsequent Attentional Problems in Children," *Pediatrics*  
* **Zimmerman & Christakis** (2007), content-specificity follow-up in *Pediatrics*  
* **Rasmussen et al.** (2016), "Relation between active mediation, exposure to Daniel Tiger's Neighborhood, and US preschoolers' social and emotional development," *Journal of Children and Media*  
* **Rasmussen et al.** (2018), "Promoting Preschoolers' Emotional Competence Through Prosocial TV and Mobile App Use," *Media Psychology*  
* **Lillard & Peterson** (2011), fast-paced fantastical programming and executive function, *Pediatrics*  
* **Christopher Kelty**, *Two Bits: The Cultural Significance of Free Software* (Duke UP, 2008\)  
* **Fred Rogers's 1969 Senate testimony** — full video at misterrogers.org; transcript at americanrhetoric.com

# Research 2

# **Research 2: Co-Viewing, Joint Media Engagement, and Family Ritual**

**Serves:** Chapter 2 of *Grammars of the Living World* **Compiled:** March 2026 **License:** CC BY-SA 4.0

---

## **1\. The Christakis/Radesky Corpus: What We Know**

Dimitri Christakis (Seattle Children's Research Institute, editor-in-chief of *JAMA Pediatrics*) and Jenny Radesky (University of Michigan Medical School, co-Medical Director of the AAP Center of Excellence on Social Media and Youth Mental Health) are the two most consequential researchers on children's media and development. Together they authored the American Academy of Pediatrics' 2016 policy statement *Media and Young Minds*, which established the current framework for pediatric guidance on screen use.

### **The Core Framework**

Their 2016 AAP policy statement established three principles that remain the consensus:

1. **Content matters more than quantity.** The association between early television and attentional problems is specific to non-educational viewing before age 3\. Educational programming shows no adverse cognitive outcomes and may actively benefit development (Zimmerman & Christakis, 2007).

2. **Co-viewing transforms the medium.** Parent-child shared media use changes the nature of the experience from passive consumption to active learning. The AAP explicitly emphasizes "the importance of parent–child shared media use and allowing the child time to take part in unstructured play."

3. **Context shapes everything.** Interrelated factors — parental co-viewing, type of content (educational/prosocial vs. adult-intended), length of engagement, and the child's developmental stage — collectively determine whether screen use helps or harms.

### **Radesky's Evolution: From Parental Guidance to Systemic Design**

In a 2025 interview, Radesky described her intellectual journey: "Dimitri Christakis and I were the authors of the early childhood guidelines. We were focused on, 'What can parents do around media to make it healthier for kids?' Right now I'm much more flipped and focused on, 'What could the tech industry do and policymakers do to make this easier for families across the board?'"

This shift — from individual behavior change to systemic design reform — mirrors the book's own argument. The crisis cannot be solved by telling parents to put down their phones. The crisis requires building infrastructure that serves families rather than exploiting them. Radesky's 2022 study in *JAMA Network Open* documented the prevalence of manipulative design patterns in apps used by children, establishing empirically what the book argues philosophically: the attention economy is structurally hostile to children's development.

---

## **2\. The "Absent Presence" and Technoference**

### **The Concept**

"Absent presence" was first described by sociologist Kenneth Gergen in 2002: the act of being physically present but having one's mind elsewhere due to communication or content from mobile phones. The term captured a new social phenomenon that has since been refined into the research concept of "technoference" — technology-based interference in face-to-face interactions.

### **The Key Research**

**McDaniel & Radesky (2018)** conducted the landmark study: 170 U.S. families (children averaging 3 years old) reported on technology interruptions during parent-child interactions. Using Actor Partner Interdependence Modeling, they found that problematic parental digital technology use predicted greater technoference, and maternal technoference predicted both mothers' and fathers' reports of child externalizing and internalizing behavior problems.

The numbers are sobering. Mothers reported technology interrupting interactions with their infant or young child *at least sometimes* in: 65% of playtime, 36% of book reading, 26% of mealtime, 26% of bedtime routines, and even 22% of discipline and limit setting. A phone-tracking study found parents spend 5.12 hours per day on smartphones and are engaged with their device 27% of the time they spend with their infant.

### **The Still Face Paradigm Applied to Phones**

Researchers have adapted the classic "Still Face Paradigm" (Tronick et al., 1978\) — where a parent stops interacting and goes expressionless — by substituting phone use for the still-face segment. During the phone-use segment, infant bids for attention increased, negative emotions increased, and positive emotions decreased. Infants were reacting to and disturbed by their parent's disengagement with a device. The phone, in other words, produces the same developmental disruption as the experimentally induced emotional withdrawal that has been studied for decades.

### **Longitudinal and Bidirectional Effects**

A 2024 cohort study in Calgary, Canada, examined bidirectional associations between emerging adolescents' perceptions of parental technoference and their anxiety, depression, attention difficulties, and hyperactivity symptoms across ages 9–11. The associations were bidirectional: technoference predicted worsening mental health, but children's mental health difficulties also predicted increasing parental withdrawal into technology — a feedback loop.

Preadolescents (ages 9–13) who perceived higher parental technoference rated their relationship with parents significantly higher in conflict and lower in companionship and emotional support.

### **The Couple Relationship Spillover**

Technoference doesn't only affect parent-child dyads. It is linked to decreased relationship satisfaction, lower quality interactions, greater conflict, decreased closeness, and lower coparenting quality in couple relationships. Since the quality of the couple relationship spills into parenting quality and children's felt security, technoference operates at multiple levels of the family system simultaneously.

**Key insight for the book:** Technoference is not a problem of individual willpower. It is a structural feature of how devices are designed — optimized to capture attention through notifications, variable reward schedules, and infinite scroll. Parents report that phone use is something they consider modifiable but perceive low control over. This supports the book's argument that the crisis requires design-level solutions (platforms built for contemplation rather than engagement), not merely individual behavior change.

---

## **3\. Read-Aloud vs. Screen Stories: What the Brain Shows**

### **The Neuroimaging Evidence**

A 2025 study published in *Developmental Science* used functional near-infrared spectroscopy (fNIRS) to observe 28 preschoolers' brains during both live book reading by a human and audio-recorded stories paired with screen images. The findings:

* **Live book reading** produced greater activation in the **right hemisphere**, particularly in regions involved in social understanding (temporal parietal junction, superior temporal gyrus) — the brain areas responsible for processing others' emotions, intentions, and shared attention.  
* **Screen-based storytelling** produced more balanced bilateral activity, relying less on social engagement networks.

The researchers concluded: "The social interaction involved in live book reading may help build neural pathways important for communication and empathy." The child's brain responds differently when a human being is present — reading with them, not to them — because the social interaction recruits brain systems that solitary screen viewing does not activate.

### **The Vocabulary Gap**

Storybooks contain 50% more rare words than prime-time television and even college students' conversations. A child hearing "giraffe," "perseverance," or "magnificent" during bedtime reading is encountering vocabulary they would rarely encounter in spoken language or screen media. Repeated readings of the same book help children ages 3–5 learn novel words, with each repetition allowing the child to focus on different elements — pictures one night, words the next, narrative structure the next.

### **The Cluster-Randomized Trial in Brazil**

A 2017 study in Boa Vista, Brazil (566 parent-child dyads in 12 childcare clusters) tested a program promoting parent-child read-aloud. Nine months later, intervention families showed significantly greater cognitive stimulation, higher quality reading interactions, and children scored higher on receptive vocabulary, working memory, and IQ than controls receiving standard childcare alone. Effect sizes were moderate and meaningful (d \= 0.33–0.57).

### **The 2026 Bedtime Reading Study**

A February 2026 study recruited 38 families in Virginia with children ages 6–8. After just 14 bedtimes of reading together, children showed significant improvement in cognitive empathy and creative thinking — regardless of whether parents paused to ask questions or read straight through. The act of shared reading itself was the active ingredient, not the specific pedagogical technique. The neuroscientist-author noted: "These 15 minutes of reading aren't just about preparing kids to sleep or teaching them to decode words. They're building neural pathways for understanding others and imagining possibilities."

**Key insight for the book:** Reading aloud is not merely an educational activity. It is a contemplative practice — a shared encounter with story that exercises the child's capacity for empathy, imagination, and social cognition. The brain evidence confirms what Rogers and McFarland intuited: the medium matters less than the relational context. A screen with a present parent may work; a screen without a parent activates different neural systems entirely. This is the neurological basis for Jongu's design philosophy of shared practice over passive consumption.

---

## **4\. Children's Preference for Repetition: The "Again\! Again\!" Phenomenon**

### **Why Children Demand the Same Story**

A preference for familiarity over novelty is one of the most robust findings in developmental psychology. It begins before birth — fetuses in the third trimester develop preferences for familiar flavors, sounds, and stories read to them through the womb (DeCasper & Spence, 1986). Newborns prefer their mother's voice and their native language within hours of birth.

This preference for the familiar reflects an early stage in the learning process. As Harvard neuroscientist Charles Nelson III explains: "Brain wiring is made possible by repetition. If you have a whole group of neurons that have to start to develop into a circuit, they need to be fired over and over. Neurons that fire together, wire together."

### **What Repetition Accomplishes**

The developmental science identifies multiple functions:

**Memory consolidation.** Younger children require longer to encode information and forget faster than older children. A 1.5-year-old remembers a sequence of new actions for about two weeks; a 2-year-old remembers for three months. Being read the same story four times rather than two significantly improved 1.5- and 2-year-olds' accuracy in reproducing target actions. Two-dimensional sources (books, screens) are harder to learn from than direct experience — repetition bridges this gap.

**Vocabulary building.** Repeated readings help children ages 3–5 learn novel words. Each pass allows focus on different elements — pictures, words, story flow, emotional content. Research by Horst, Parsons, & Bryan (2011) demonstrated that contextual repetition promotes word learning from storybooks.

**Emotional regulation.** Predictability is comforting. When a child knows what comes next in a story, expectations are met, which provides "a safe harbor in an often unpredictable day." The cognitive and emotional functions in the brain work in tandem — children learn better when their physical need for safety and connection is met.

**Mastery and agency.** Rebecca Parlakian (Zero to Three) notes: "So often I hear adults saying, well, how do we motivate children to learn? You do not have to motivate children to learn. They are driven to master the world around them, and they do that through repetition." The child who has memorized the book and can "read" it to the parent is experiencing agency — they have mastered something, and the positive parental response reinforces both the learning and the relationship.

### **The Blue's Clues Principle**

*Blue's Clues* was deliberately designed to harness learning from repetition: the same episode aired five consecutive days before a new one. Research showed that five days of viewing the same episode increased 3-to-5-year-olds' comprehension and increased their interaction with the program, compared to single viewing. Children were learning not just content but *how to learn from the medium* — transferring knowledge to new episodes and series.

**Key insight for the book:** The child's demand for repetition is not a quirk to be managed. It is a developmental strategy. It is also, remarkably, what grammars do. The I Ching practitioner who casts the same hexagram repeatedly over months is doing something structurally identical to the child who demands the same bedtime story: encountering the same finite symbolic vocabulary over and over, each time bringing a different developmental stage, a different emotional context, a different capacity for interpretation. "Grammars are humanity's oldest contemplative technologies" — and the child who says "Again\! Again\!" is already practicing.

---

## **5\. Technoference and the Serve-and-Return Disruption**

### **What Serve-and-Return Means**

The concept of "serve and return" — drawn from Harvard's Center on the Developing Child — describes the fundamental interaction pattern between caregivers and children: the child "serves" (a babble, a gesture, a look), and the adult "returns" (with attention, a word, a responsive action). This back-and-forth builds neural architecture. It is the developmental mechanism through which attachment, language, and self-regulation develop.

### **How Phones Disrupt It**

Christakis has directly connected his screen research to the serve-and-return framework. In a 2025 podcast, he described a study where children were playing with a regular toy, an electronic toy, or a highly engaging app, and a research assistant tried to get the child's attention at a predetermined point. Children's attention was much more difficult to command when playing with engaging apps. But the disruption runs both ways — when parents are absorbed in their phones, they fail to "return" the child's "serve."

As Christakis noted: "What we're finding is that media have the potential, the very real potential, to interrupt that serve and return, to diminish those opportunities. And those opportunities are so essential to all aspects of child development."

The mediating use of devices to calm upset children (Radesky et al., 2016\) is particularly concerning: when parents use mobile technology as a pacifier, children miss opportunities to develop their own self-regulation skills. A 2023 longitudinal study in *JAMA Pediatrics* found that using mobile devices to calm children aged 3–5 predicted greater emotional reactivity and poorer executive functioning over time.

---

## **6\. Can Curated Shared Playlists Function as Family Ritual?**

This is the central question Research 2 was designed to address — and the research converges on a conditional yes.

### **What the Evidence Supports**

1. **Shared media use with parental mediation produces developmental benefits.** The Daniel Tiger research (Research 1\) demonstrated this for prosocial television. The neuroimaging research shows that a human presence during storytelling recruits social brain networks that solitary viewing does not.

2. **Repetition within a shared context is developmentally productive.** The Blue's Clues model, the bedtime reading research, and the vocabulary acquisition studies all confirm that repeated encounters with the same material in a relational context produce learning, emotional regulation, and attachment.

3. **The ritual quality matters.** Bedtime reading functions as ritual: it is repeated, it occurs in a dedicated time and space, it involves physical closeness, it has a beginning and end, and it creates shared symbolic reference points ("Remember when we read about..."). These are the structural features of ritual — and they map directly onto what grammars provide.

### **What the Evidence Warns Against**

1. **Passive consumption without relational context does not work.** The Daniel Tiger results were only significant for empathy when parents actively discussed the show. Screens without present adults activate different neural systems.

2. **Design features matter.** Autoplay, variable reward, infinite scroll, notifications, and gamification all undermine the contemplative quality that makes shared media ritual-like. A "shared playlist" that algorithmically recommends the next item is fundamentally different from one that a family has curated together and returns to repeatedly.

3. **The medium must not displace the relationship.** The technoference research is unambiguous: when the device becomes the primary attentional object rather than the shared practice, developmental harm follows.

### **Design Principles for Jongu**

The research suggests that a curated, shared grammar playlist could function as family ritual if it satisfies these conditions:

* **Human curation, not algorithmic recommendation.** The parent creates the playlist — selecting folk tales, tarot reflections, I Ching readings, meditation exercises — based on their knowledge of their family's needs.  
* **Repetition is built in.** The same grammars are returned to, like bedtime stories. Each return brings new developmental capacity, new emotional context, new interpretive depth.  
* **The device facilitates the relationship, not the reverse.** No notifications. No autoplay. No engagement metrics. The platform holds your question without answering it.  
* **Co-creation is possible.** The parent and child can contribute to the grammar together — a drawing, a journal entry, a response to a casting. The playlist is not consumed but participated in.  
* **Exportable, deletable, private by default.** The family's ritual space is their own.

---

## **7\. The Decline of Shared Family Narrative**

### **The Read-Aloud Crisis**

In 2024, only 51% of families read aloud to their very young children, while only 37% read aloud to children ages 6–8. A third of parents said they wished they had more time. The decline is not primarily about attitudes — most parents know reading matters — but about the structural conditions of family life: exhaustion, time pressure, and the competing pull of devices that are designed to capture attention.

### **What Is Lost**

When shared reading declines, what is lost is not merely literacy preparation. It is:

* **Shared symbolic vocabulary.** The family that has read *The Velveteen Rabbit* together shares a reference point for conversations about love, authenticity, and what it means to be "real." Without shared stories, families lack this common symbolic ground.  
* **Contemplative time together.** Reading aloud requires slowing down, being physically close, and being fully present. In a world of technoference, this may be the only 15 minutes of the day when both parent and child are attending to the same thing at the same time.  
* **The neural architecture for empathy.** The 2025 fNIRS study shows that the social brain networks recruited by live shared reading are not activated by solitary screen use. The decline of read-aloud is not just a cultural loss but a neurological one.  
* **Ritual structure.** Bedtime reading has the structure of ritual — repeated, bounded, embodied, shared. When it disappears, what replaces it? Usually, individual screen use in separate rooms.

**Key insight for the book:** The decline of shared family narrative is not a separate phenomenon from the crisis of mythic belonging. It is the same crisis, experienced at the smallest scale. If a civilization cannot maintain shared stories at the level of a single family's bedtime, what hope does it have for maintaining them at the level of a community, a nation, or a species? Jongu's "shared grammar playlists" are an attempt to provide infrastructure for this most fundamental practice — not as a replacement for books and bedtime, but as a complementary practice that extends the ritual logic of shared reading into the digital medium.

---

## **8\. Connections to the Book's Architecture**

### **For Chapter 2 (The Crisis)**

The research in this document provides the empirical spine of Chapter 2\. The structure could move from the broad (the attention economy's structural hostility to children) through the specific (technoference disrupting serve-and-return in individual families) to the hopeful (what shared media practices look like when they work — Daniel Tiger, bedtime reading, Blue's Clues repetition).

The chapter's emotional center should be the neuroimaging finding: a child's brain literally functions differently when a human being is present during storytelling. This is the scientific basis for the book's central claim that grammars are relational — they hold your question without answering it, but only if someone is holding you.

### **For Chapter 8 (The Commons / Grammars of the Living World)**

The "shared grammar playlist" concept arrives here as the practical application. The design principles derived from the research — human curation, built-in repetition, device-as-facilitator, co-creation, privacy-by-default — are the product design consequences of taking the developmental science seriously.

The closing image: a parent and child, sitting together, casting a tarot card or reading a folk tale from a curated grammar, returning to it again tomorrow. "Again\! Again\!" The child is already practicing.

---

# Research 4

# **Research 4: Bayo Akomolafe and Vanessa Andreotti**

**Serves:** Chapter 7 ("The Trap of Solving") of *Grammars of the Living World* **Compiled:** March 2026 **License:** CC BY-SA 4.0

---

## **1\. Andreotti's *Hospicing Modernity*: The Core Argument**

Vanessa de Oliveira Andreotti (Dean of the Faculty of Education, University of Victoria; former Canada Research Chair in Race, Inequalities and Global Change) published *Hospicing Modernity: Facing Humanity's Wrongs and the Implications for Social Activism* in September 2021\. The book's central metaphor gives it its force: modernity is not a problem to be solved but a dying patient to be accompanied.

### **What Modernity Is**

Andreotti defines modernity as "a ubiquitous story of linear progress, development, evolution, and civilization that is all around us and that informs the ways we think, the ways we imagine things, the ways we hope, the ways we desire. That also informs our neurobiology in many ways: where we source pleasure, what we're afraid of." Modernity is not merely an economic system or a political ideology — it colonizes the unconscious. Its "most important area of domination was the mental universe of the colonised" (echoing Ngũgĩ wa Thiong'o).

### **The Hospice Metaphor**

The book's radical move is refusing to propose an alternative to modernity. Instead, it asks how we can accompany modernity's death — within ourselves and around us — with "compassion, curiosity and dignity." Andreotti writes: "We can soften the crash, but not prevent it: disenchantment and disillusionment will happen, one way or another. But it is your call whether to move toward it now or not, slowly or not, with assistance or not."

This is not passivity. It is a reorientation of agency — from fixing to accompanying, from solving to holding. The book explicitly invites readers to "expand our collective capacity to hold space for difficult and painful things."

### **The Five Modern-Colonial E's**

Andreotti identifies five orientations that modernity produces in its subjects: Entitlements, Exceptionalism, Exaltation, Emancipation, and Enmeshment in "low-intensity struggle activism." The last is particularly relevant for the book: activism that reproduces the very logic it claims to critique — the impulse to identify a problem, propose a solution, implement it, evaluate it, and move on. This is the "trap of solving" that Chapter 7 addresses.

### **The Exercises**

*Hospicing Modernity* is part philosophical treatise, part workbook. It includes numerous "check your bus" exercises — invitations for the reader to examine their own complicity in the structures they critique. This pedagogical method is directly relevant to Jongu's design: the platform holds your question without answering it. The grammar is a contemplative technology for sitting with, not solving.

---

## **2\. Akomolafe's "Postactivism": When Solutions Become Part of the Crisis**

Bayo Akomolafe (PhD, psychology; Yoruba heritage; founder of The Emergence Network; adjunct at Pacifica Graduate Institute) is a philosopher, psychologist, poet, and public intellectual whose work centers on a single provocative question: **"What if the way we respond to the crisis is part of the crisis?"**

### **The Core Concepts**

**Postactivism** is Akomolafe's central concept. It challenges the binary thinking of traditional activism (us vs. them, oppressor vs. oppressed, problem vs. solution) and questions whether conventional activism perpetuates the same logics that produced the issues it aims to solve. Postactivism "shifts the focus from merely doing (actions and outcomes) to being (ways of being and relating)."

**Onto-fugitivity** describes the dynamic, constantly shifting nature of being. Akomolafe argues that colonialism imposed rigid, hierarchical structures on our understanding of existence. Being is not static but "fugitive" — always escaping categorization. This challenges the conventional identities on which both oppression and resistance are built.

**The "transjective"** is Akomolafe's term for the interstitial space between subject and object. Using the image of a footprint: "the footprint doesn't belong to the mud nor to the foot, but the interstitial space that acquiesced to them to come together." This space — not objective nor subjective but transjective — is where postactivist practice occurs.

### **Psychology Creates Rather Than Describes Reality**

Akomolafe's most relevant claim for the book is that the therapeutic and diagnostic frameworks of Western psychology are not neutral descriptions of reality but active constructions that shape what they claim to observe. He states: "Trauma is already the political project of dissociation from our co-emergence with ecology. The very notion is a colonial imperial settlement ethic — it's about preserving my coddled, isolated, separated mind." And: "A clean bill of health is no less ideological than the Bible."

This critique of psychology-as-world-making maps directly onto the book's critique of the attention economy. Co-Star doesn't describe your astrological reality — it constructs an attention-capturing version of it. Headspace doesn't describe meditation — it constructs a commodified version of it. The question is always: what world does this practice create, and whose interests does that world serve?

### **Yoruba Cosmology as Framework**

Akomolafe draws heavily on Yoruba indigenous cosmologies, particularly the figure of Eshu (the trickster Orisha) as a model for postactivist practice. Eshu doesn't solve problems directly — he introduces chaos, misdirection, and play into situations that appear stuck. The trickster's logic is: when the direct path fails, the indirect path opens. This is the logic of the grammar — not answering your question but reframing the field within which questions arise.

---

## **3\. Intellectual Lineage: Where They Draw From**

### **Andreotti's Sources**

Andreotti draws from: decolonial theory (Quijano's "coloniality of power," Mignolo's "border thinking"), indigenous cosmologies (particularly Andean traditions from her own Brazilian-Andean background), posthumanism, affect theory, and contemplative practice. Her work with the "Gesturing Towards Decolonial Futures" arts/research collective grounds her theoretical work in participatory practice.

### **Akomolafe's Sources**

Akomolafe draws from: Yoruba cosmology and Ifá, posthumanism (Donna Haraway, Karen Barad), new materialism, Frantz Fanon's post-colonial psychoanalysis, process philosophy, and contemplative traditions. He was trained as a clinical psychologist (Covenant University, Nigeria) before moving into philosophy and public intellectual work.

### **Shared Lineage**

Both draw on: the recognition that modernity's epistemological framework cannot be used to solve problems that the same framework created; indigenous knowledge systems as sources of alternative ontology (not just content but structure); the critique of the autonomous, sovereign individual as a colonial fiction; and the insistence that embodied, relational, situated practice matters more than abstract theory.

---

## **4\. Critical Reception: Who Finds Them Compelling and Who Doesn't**

### **Positive Reception**

Both thinkers have been celebrated within decolonial, posthumanist, and contemplative activist circles. *Hospicing Modernity* received endorsements from Kyle Whyte (University of Michigan), Dougald Hine (Dark Mountain Project), and Bayo Akomolafe himself, who called it "a book written for us as people who struggle with the everyday manifestations of modern power." Akomolafe has taught at Middlebury, Schumacher College, Simon Fraser, and Pacifica, and was invited as Special Envoy of the International Alliance for Localization.

### **The Scholarly Rigor Question**

The most persistent critique — and the one the book must address honestly — is that both thinkers operate in a register that blurs the line between scholarly argument and poetic/gestural performance. Akomolafe's writing is deliberately non-linear, metaphor-rich, and resistant to the kind of falsifiable claims that academic philosophy demands. Andreotti's *Hospicing Modernity* mixes memoir, philosophy, poetry, drawings, exercises, and songs.

One Goodreads reviewer captured the tension: "I've read this book twice and am still trying to settle my thoughts about it. That's because Hospicing Modernity is an unusual mix of genres and themes... I'm not sure the mix works, but I've been thinking about it for a while, which means it certainly had an impact."

The question for the book is: **does the gestural register weaken or strengthen the argument?** If modernity's deepest reflex is to identify a problem and solve it, then a text that refuses to present its argument in modernity's preferred form (linear, evidence-based, falsifiable) may be performing its argument rather than merely stating it. But this defense can also become circular — any criticism of the work's rigor can be deflected as itself a manifestation of the colonial epistemology being critiqued.

### **What Claims Are Evidenced vs. Poetic**

**Evidenced claims (Andreotti):** The historical record of colonial violence and its epistemological dimensions is well-documented (building on Ngũgĩ, Fanon, Linda Tuhiwai Smith, Quijano). The "five modern-colonial E's" framework has been developed through years of pedagogical practice with NGO workers, teachers, and activists. The exercises have been tested in workshops internationally.

**Evidenced claims (Akomolafe):** The argument that activist frameworks can reproduce the logics they critique has strong support in social movement theory. The Yoruba cosmological material draws on a genuine scholarly tradition (Wande Abimbola, Kola Abimbola, Olupona — all documented in your Ifá research).

**Poetic/gestural claims:** That "modernity is dying" (Andreotti) — this is a metaphorical framing, not an empirically testable claim. That "the way we respond to the crisis is part of the crisis" (Akomolafe) — this is a heuristic, powerful for reorientation but not falsifiable in the scientific sense. That Western psychology "creates" rather than "describes" reality — this is a philosophical position (constructivism) with strong support in some traditions and strong opposition in others.

---

## **5\. Comparison with More Rigorously Grounded Critiques**

The book's Chapter 7 needs Andreotti and Akomolafe, but it also needs thinkers who make similar arguments with more conventional scholarly grounding, to triangulate the claim.

### **Ngũgĩ wa Thiong'o (b. 1938\)**

*Decolonising the Mind: The Politics of Language in African Literature* (1986) remains the foundational text on epistemological colonialism. Ngũgĩ's argument is concrete and specific: colonialism's "most important area of domination was the mental universe of the colonised, the control through culture, of how people perceived themselves and their relationship to the world." His focus on language as the vehicle of mental colonization — "the bullet was the means of physical subjugation; the language was the means of spiritual subjugation" — is empirically grounded in the history of colonial education policy in Kenya.

Ngũgĩ enacted his argument: he abandoned English in his fiction writing and committed to writing in Gĩkũyũ, was imprisoned without trial by the Kenyan regime, and went into exile. The argument is not merely theoretical — it has a body, a practice, a price. This concreteness is what gives Ngũgĩ's work authority that more abstract decolonial theory sometimes lacks.

**Relevance for the book:** Ngũgĩ shows that decolonizing the mind requires not just critique but alternative practice — actually doing something differently, not just theorizing about doing something differently. This maps directly onto the book's argument: the platform doesn't just critique the attention economy, it provides an alternative.

### **Linda Tuhiwai Smith (b. 1950\)**

*Decolonizing Methodologies: Research and Indigenous Peoples* (1999, 3rd ed. 2021\) is the foundational text on indigenous research methodology. A Māori scholar at the University of Auckland, Tuhiwai Smith builds on Ngũgĩ's thesis to argue that "decolonization is a long-term process involving the cultural, linguistic and psychological divesting of colonial parameters." The Western tradition of knowledge is grounded in positivism — the notion that research is objective and value-free. Tuhiwai Smith introduces Kaupapa Māori as an alternative epistemology: "We have a different epistemological tradition which frames the way we see the world."

**Relevance for the book:** Tuhiwai Smith provides the methodological framework for what ethical engagement with indigenous knowledge systems looks like in practice — exactly the question Jongu faces with initiated traditions like Ifá. Her concept of research accountability to indigenous communities maps onto the CARE Principles the book already uses.

### **Boaventura de Sousa Santos (b. 1940\)**

*Epistemologies of the South: Justice Against Epistemicide* (2014) provides the most rigorous theoretical account of what Santos calls "epistemicide" — the systematic destruction of non-Western ways of knowing. Santos advocates "pluriversal" epistemologies: not replacing Western knowledge with indigenous knowledge, but creating horizontal dialogue between multiple epistemic traditions. His concept of "abyssal thinking" — the division of social reality into two sides, one visible and one invisible — describes the mechanism by which non-Western knowledge is rendered non-existent.

**Relevance for the book:** Santos's "epistemicide" is exactly what Chapter 5 ("Survival") documents — the Qin burning, the destruction of Maya codices, the suppression of Ifá under colonialism. His "pluriversal" framework provides the theoretical basis for a platform that hosts I Ching next to Tarot next to Ifá next to Dhammapada without reducing them to equivalence.

---

## **6\. How Their Critique Applies (or Doesn't) to Digital Commons**

### **Where It Applies**

Andreotti and Akomolafe's critique applies powerfully to Jongu in several ways:

**The platform cannot solve the crisis of mythic belonging without reproducing the logic that created it.** If modernity's deepest reflex is problem → solution → implementation, then building a platform to "solve" the meaning crisis is already caught in the trap. The book's Chapter 7 argument — that the impulse to solve is part of the problem — draws directly from Akomolafe's question.

**The "low-intensity struggle activism" critique.** Andreotti's fifth "E" (Enmeshment) describes activism that makes people feel good about themselves without actually changing anything. An open-source contemplative platform could easily become this — a way for progressive, educated people to feel they're doing something meaningful while the structural conditions of the attention economy remain unchanged.

**The constructivism of technology.** If psychology creates rather than describes reality (Akomolafe), then a platform creates rather than merely serves contemplative practice. Every design decision — what gets a button, what gets foregrounded, what is measurable — constructs a specific version of contemplative life and suppresses others.

### **Where It Doesn't Fully Apply**

**The recursive public is already the answer to the critique.** Kelty's concept — a community that builds and maintains the very infrastructure through which it makes meaning — is precisely the kind of practice-based, non-solutionist, continuously modifiable approach that both Andreotti and Akomolafe call for. Jongu doesn't claim to have the answer. It creates conditions and sees what the community does with them.

**The "gesture" framing.** The book already uses the term "gesture toward adaptive futures" — borrowed from the same intellectual milieu — to describe what the platform attempts. Not a solution but a wager. Not certainty but courage.

**Some things need to be built.** The deepest tension between Akomolafe/Andreotti and Jongu is that the platform requires building — writing code, designing interfaces, making decisions, deploying infrastructure. Postactivism can become a kind of philosophical paralysis when taken to its extreme: if every response to the crisis is part of the crisis, then no response is possible. The book's answer (drawing on Tillich, Palmer, Wallis) is that inaction is also an action, and that the courage to build something imperfect — treating every version as a draft — is itself a contemplative practice.

---

## **7\. The Scholarly Rigor Question: What the Book Should Do**

Chapter 7 should present Andreotti and Akomolafe not as authorities but as the most articulate contemporary voices for a position the book takes seriously: that the impulse to solve is itself part of the problem. The chapter should:

1. **State their arguments clearly and sympathetically** — the "trap of solving" thesis, the hospice metaphor, postactivism as an alternative to binary activism.

2. **Triangulate with more conventionally grounded thinkers** — Ngũgĩ on linguistic colonization, Tuhiwai Smith on research methodology, Santos on epistemicide — to show that the decolonial critique is not merely gestural but has empirical and methodological substance.

3. **Acknowledge the circular defense problem honestly.** The book should say, in its own voice, something like: "The risk of this position is that it becomes unfalsifiable — any criticism can be deflected as a manifestation of the colonial logic being critiqued. The reader should hold these arguments as they hold everything else in this book — as questions, not as authorities."

4. **Show how the recursive public addresses the critique practically.** Not by solving the trap of solving, but by building infrastructure that remains modifiable — that treats every version as a draft, that privileges the community's capacity to adapt over the founder's capacity to plan.

---

## **Key Sources**

* **Vanessa Andreotti**, *Hospicing Modernity: Facing Humanity's Wrongs and the Implications for Social Activism* (North Atlantic Books, 2021\)  
* **Bayo Akomolafe**, *These Wilds Beyond Our Fences: Letters to My Daughter on Humanity's Search for Home* (North Atlantic Books, 2017\)  
* **Akomolafe**, "Postactivism" — talks at Garrison Institute (2023), CIIS (2024), Emergence Network  
* **Ngũgĩ wa Thiong'o**, *Decolonising the Mind: The Politics of Language in African Literature* (Heinemann, 1986\)  
* **Linda Tuhiwai Smith**, *Decolonizing Methodologies: Research and Indigenous Peoples* (Zed Books, 1999/2021)  
* **Boaventura de Sousa Santos**, *Epistemologies of the South: Justice Against Epistemicide* (Paradigm Publishers, 2014\)  
* **Gesturing Towards Decolonial Futures** collective — decolonialfutures.net  
* **The Emergence Network** — emergencenetwork.org

---
